    Notes:

    • Concrete representation, p. 46

       

      "when it comes to the interpretation of a picture's concrete content, no special interpretational (sic) skills should be required beyond those required by reality itself". p.46

      Discrepancies between reality and representation: light and color/depth/object recognition

      Light and color

      Gombrich, 1960, Art and Illusion: "A viewer who had never seen a picture before might require some adjustment to this unfamiliar reduction in the range of brightness, but the adjustment should be rapid and unproblematic, because the interpretational task posed by the picture calls for mental skills which the viewer has already developed in interactions with the real visual sorround" [Check Gibson, 1954]

      "With regard to this aspect of pictures, then, Gombrich analysis can be seen as an argument against the notion of visual literacy, if the term is taken to imply the existence of a picture-specific set of interpretational skills that viewers have to learn through extensive experience with pictures.

      Visual information processing (Marr, 1982)

      According to Marr, the absence of color might not be an issue for identification, light might be a visuogrammatic element, but not necessarily color, because according to Marr color doesn't preclude perception as light does.

      Depth

      Distance between a viewer eyes and the position of any point in the visual field. p.51-52

      What informs visual (2D) depth:

      1. Binocular disparity (stereoscopy)
      2. Motion parallax
      3. Occlusion [limited because it always happen in relation with another object and it cannot inform how far is the object from the viewer or the reference object]
      4. Texture gradients (Gibson)
      5. Contours: "The first step in the brain extraction of depth information from the retinal image consist of the derivation of outlines of objects and surface discontinuities.
      6. Shading 

      According to Marr, depth can be inferred from any of these factors. p. 52

      Marr: Depth is particularly important because "the inference of depth precedes identification" (p.53). The identification of a solid body comes before the establishment of its identity based on contour or outline because both of these (contour or outline) change every time.

      In ancient Egypt, Greece, and probably in many other ancient cultures the main depth cue is occlusion.

      Two more cues (mainly in paintings) Linear perspective, relative size.

      Object recognition

      Objects are recognized as three-dimensional entities, otherwise the "stock" of outlines to compare will be huge.

      Marr and Nishira: Recognition of organic forms based on structural representation (principal axes, symmetry). So, it can be a stock of "structural representations". So, according to Messaris, as long as the structure follows the model, is recognizable (like stick figures or the michelin guy, or any other kind of representation in which there are limbs, torso, etc.
      Is this structural depiction a form of evolved "visual concept"?

  • "Mind and sight": Visual literacy and the archivist

    Type Journal Article
    Author Elisabeth Kaplan
    Author Jeffrey Mifflin
    URL http://www.jstor.org/stable/41101943
    Rights Copyright © 1996 Midwest Archives Conference
    Volume 21
    Issue 2
    Pages 107-127
    Publication Archival Issues
    ISSN 1067-4993
    Date January 1, 1996
    Extra ArticleType: research-article / Full publication date: 1996 / Copyright © 1996 Midwest Archives Conference
    Journal Abbr Archival Issues
    DOI 10.2307/41101943
    Accessed 10/4/2013, 5:22:00 PM
    Library Catalog JSTOR
    Abstract Contemporary culture is increasingly captured by and reflected in visual materials. Preserving and providing intellectual access to visual records will become an increasingly important aspect of archival work as such materials proliferate and are widely available in electronic form. Visual literacy, an evolving concept best defined as the ability to understand and use images and to think and learn in terms of images, is an essential skill for archivists and researchers using visual materials. Archivists of all media should strive to increase their visual literacy because of the complex ways in which visual and "traditional" textual documents interrelate. Archivists can approach visual literacy by becoming familiar with levels of visual awareness; participating in the ongoing discourse about the nature of literacy, including the relationships between visual and textual literacy; and increasing understanding of the special characteristics of image-creating technologies as well as the conventions and modes of expression associated with particular media. Expanded visual literacy will help archivists to understand and better describe visual resources as well as traditional documents and other materials of record. The results, improved finding aids and catalog records, will keep pace with anticipated expanding requirements of the research community.
    Short Title "MIND AND SIGHT"
    Date Added 10/4/2013, 5:22:00 PM

  • A Pedagogy of Multiliteracies: Designing Social Futures

    Type Journal Article
    Author The New London Group
    URL http://hepg.metapress.com/content/17370N67V22J160U
    Volume 66
    Issue 1
    Pages 60-93
    Publication Harvard Educational Review
    Date April 1, 1996
    Abstract In this article, the New London Group presents a theoretical overview of the connections between the changing social environment facing students and teachers and a new approach to literacy pedagogy that they call "multiliteracies." The authors argue that the multiplicity of communications channels and increasing cultural and linguistic diversity in the world today call for a much broader view of literacy than portrayed by traditional language-based approaches. Multiliteracies, according to the authors, overcomes the limitations of traditional approaches by emphasizing how negotiating the multiple linguistic and cultural differences in our society is central to the pragmatics of the working, civic, and private lives of students. The authors maintain that the use of multiliteracies approaches to pedagogy will enable students to achieve the authors' twin goals for literacy learning: creating access to the evolving language of work, power, and community, and fostering the critical engagement necessary for them to design their social futures and achieve success through fulfilling employment.

  • Visual Literacy/Aesthetic Development Research: Museum-Public School Cooperation

    Type Journal Article
    Author M. Victor Alper
    URL http://www.jstor.org/stable/20715869
    Rights Copyright © 1996 University of Illinois Press
    Volume 22
    Issue 1(43)
    Pages 62-78
    Publication Visual Arts Research
    ISSN 0736-0770
    Date April 1, 1996
    Extra ArticleType: research-article / Full publication date: Spring 1996 / Copyright © 1996 University of Illinois Press
    Journal Abbr Visual Arts Research
    DOI 10.2307/20715869
    Accessed 10/4/2013, 4:28:02 PM
    Library Catalog JSTOR
    Abstract In a bold research project, New York's Museum of Modern Art and the New York City Public School system joined forces to create a visual literacy/aesthetic development program. To accomplish this goal, research was undertaken that included a methodology to ascertain the aesthetic development stages of students. This population was tested before and after experiencing a newly created art curriculum, with related activities. In addition, a select group of teachers participated in special visual literacy training workshops. These teachers also were tested to identify growth in aesthetic development stages. The article discusses the methodological approaches and the research findings. Afterward, the results of the project are evaluated in light of several conceptual and design limitations, including (a) consideration of attitudes, (b) attention to student learning styles and the need to adapt pedagogical approaches, (c) the study of age and aesthetic development, (d) the possibility of a Hawthorne Effect, and (e) the creation of a limited studio art module as part of the curriculum. This museum-public school research project provides a foundation for additional, integrated studies.
    Short Title Visual Literacy/Aesthetic Development Research

  • A Cartographic Interpretation of Visual Literacy: An Historical Perspective.

    Type Journal Article
    Author Cindy L. Kovalik
    Author Kim Lambdin
    URL http://eric.ed.gov/?id=ED408970
    Date 1997/01/00
    Extra 00001
    Accessed 1/7/2016, 10:11:45 PM
    Library Catalog eric.ed.gov
    Language en
    Abstract This paper presents findings from an historical investigation of visual literacy, a unique aspect being that the approach relied on the marriage of two disciplines--geography and history--which study change over time. Maps and their interpretation of data by cartographers tend to provide a foundational context that can illuminate past and present and establish connections between them. In this case, using a variety of sources, researchers mapped historical aspects of visual literacy with a geographic information system (GIS). Discussion and figures explore: (1) a time line of seminal works in critical viewing, the psychology of art, and visual literacy; (2) the acquisition and organization of the correspondence of Dr. Henry Ray, an important figure in the field, by Kent State University (location of his correspondents indicates pockets of interest in visual literacy); (3) government funding for educational programs in visual literacy; (4) media preservation efforts; and (5) visual

  • John Dewey's Experience and Education: Lessons for Museums

    Type Journal Article
    Author Ted Ansbacher
    URL http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/j.2151-6952.1998.tb00812.x/abstract
    Rights 1998 California Academy of Sciences
    Volume 41
    Issue 1
    Pages 36–50
    Publication Curator: The Museum Journal
    ISSN 2151-6952
    Date 1998
    Extra 00057
    DOI 10.1111/j.2151-6952.1998.tb00812.x
    Accessed 1/29/2013, 12:15:26 AM
    Library Catalog Wiley Online Library
    Language en
    Abstract John Dewey, one of the preeminent educational theorists of our time, wrote Experience and Education 60 years ago, toward the end of his career, as a review of his educational philosophy and the progressive schools it had spawned. Based on the principle that all genuine education comes about through experience, Dewey's ideas are still current and particularly relevant to the theory and practice of museum education. They are, nevertheless, not widely cited within the museum profession. This article comments on Experience and Education, a slim, readable volume, in the hope of giving it wider readership.Major ideas from each chapter of the book are summarized along with comments on their application to various museum issues. Dewey distinguishes two fundamentally opposed ideas: that education is development from within and that it is formation from without. Believing that experience is the basis for education, he basically takes the within position, although he also warns against either-or thinking. Dewey identifies two aspects of experience and two criteria for judging it which have implications for such things as setting exhibit goals, evaluating exhibits, developing exhibit content, and untangling education and entertainment. He goes on to look at problems of implementing experience-based learning such as maintaining social control, moving people from initial impulses to more purposeful inquiry, and developing organized subject matter from individual experiences. All of this applies to museums as well as schools.
    Short Title John Dewey's Experience and Education

  • Visual aspects of media literacy

    Type Journal Article
    Author P Messaris
    URL http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/j.1460-2466.1998.tb02738.x/abstract
    Volume 48
    Issue 1
    Pages 70–80
    Publication Journal of Communication
    ISSN 1460-2466
    Date 1998
    DOI 10.1111/j.1460-2466.1998.tb02738.x
    Accessed 7/4/2013, 10:47:45 AM
    Library Catalog Wiley Online Library
    Language en
    Abstract A central component of media literacy should be an understanding of the representational conventions through which the users of media create and share meanings. This paper analyzes the representational conventions of visual communications. It distinguishes between semantic and syntactic conventions and focuses on those characteristics that most sharply differentiate visual language from other modes of communication. It examines the impact of visual literacy on viewers' cognitive growth and on their development as informed consumers of visual media.

  • Beyond Visual Literacy: Omniphasism, A Theory of Balance (Part One of Three)

    Type Journal Article
    Author Rick Williams
    URL http://search.ebscohost.com/login.aspx?direct=true&db=ufh&AN=31444176&site=ehost-live&scope=site
    Volume 19
    Issue 2
    Pages 159-178
    Publication Journal of Visual Literacy
    ISSN 1051144X
    Date October 1999
    Journal Abbr Journal of Visual Literacy
    Accessed 5/6/2014, 10:15:25 PM
    Library Catalog EBSCOhost
    Abstract The first part of this article integrates the author's original work with a synthesis of the work of major cognitive and visual scholars. The author develops a historical and contemporary theoretical basis for Intuitive Intelligence as the primary equal and complementary cognitive paradigm to our Rational Intelligence system. Further, the theory establishes Visual Intelligence as a primary Intuitive Intelligence. Omniphasism means "all in balance" and defines the concept that both Rational and Intuitive Intelligneces are equally significant to whole-mind cognitive development.

  • The Roots of Visual Literacy: Reflections on an Historical Perspective

    Type Journal Article
    Author Teun Velders
    URL http://search.ebscohost.com/login.aspx?direct=true&db=ehh&AN=31418867&site=ehost-live&scope=site
    Volume 20
    Issue 1
    Pages 1-8
    Publication Journal of Visual Literacy
    ISSN 1051144X
    Date Spring 2000
    Call Number 31418867
    Journal Abbr Journal of Visual Literacy
    Accessed 7/9/2013, 5:25:01 PM
    Library Catalog EBSCOhost
    Abstract This paper examines the meaning of the term "visual literacy" and its relationship to media education and the environment. To a certain extent everyone who has learned to look is visually literate. The historical roots of the term trace back through time immemorial as the concept has been described repeatedly in classic literature, by scientists, and by artists. The author traces his own beginnings in the journey of learning to look and in so doing, leaches us all to reflect upon the meaning of visual literacy in another way.
    Short Title The Roots of Visual Literacy
    Date Added 7/9/2013, 5:25:01 PM
    Modified 1/7/2016, 4:11:49 PM

    Tags:

    • AUDIOVISUAL education
    • VISUAL aids
    • Visual education
    • visual learning
    • Visual literacy
    • Visual Perception

    Notes:

    • The history of visual communication goes back to the cave paintings
      30,000 years ago; the description of it only 2,500 years. Here I consider Herodotus' (484 B.C.) descriptions as the first manifestations of art description; Plato followed in the next century. If we want to study the parameters of "visual literacy" we have to go back to the roots, the artists, the creators of images, and the conformation on their view on reality by description. The artists delivered to us the visuals that are the subject of studies in visual communication. What the eye of the artist sees tums into a manifestation of reality by his hands. This way of thinking, visual thinking, not in concepts but in meaningful lines, forms, and colors, is active beholding cognition. Understanding these aims and limits of "visual literacy" should be the central to a visualy literate population. If "visual literacy" will be a basic skill for media education (and it has to be in my opinion), the history of visual communication is a basic skill for "visual literacy."In this way we can say that visual literacy is 2,500 years old (as a skill) and 30 years young (as a term).

  • Beyond Visual Literacy Part Two Visual Illiteracy and Intuitive Visual Persuasion The Academy and the Media

    Type Journal Article
    Author Rick Williams
    URL http://search.ebscohost.com/login.aspx?direct=true&db=ufh&AN=31418877&site=ehost-live&scope=site
    Volume 20
    Issue 1
    Pages 111-124
    Publication Journal of Visual Literacy
    ISSN 1051144X
    Date Spring 2000
    Journal Abbr Journal of Visual Literacy
    Accessed 5/6/2014, 10:36:11 PM
    Library Catalog EBSCOhost
    Abstract In Part One of Beyond Visual Literacy, 1 developed a theory of cognitive balance that explored a parallel and complementary relationship between the two primary cognitive processing systems that use Rational and Intuitive Intelligences as independent, but integrated cognitive processes. I also developed the concepts that Visual Intelligence is a primary Intuitive Intelligence and that a cultural, economic, educational, and scientific bias against the development of Intuitive Intelligences has created an Intuitive Illiteracy in our society. In Part Two, I will address the role of a Rationally-biased educational system in the proliferation of Visual Intuitive Illiteracy, and the subsequent role of the media as the educators and exploiters of Visual Intuitive Intelligence.

  • Through a Class Darkly: Visual Literacy in the Classroom

    Type Journal Article
    Author Deborah L. Begoray
    URL http://www.jstor.org/stable/1602201
    Rights Copyright © 2001 Canadian Society for the Study of Education
    Volume 26
    Issue 2
    Pages 201-217
    Publication Canadian Journal of Education / Revue canadienne de l'éducation
    ISSN 0380-2361
    Date January 1, 2001
    Extra ArticleType: research-article / Full publication date: 2001 / Copyright © 2001 Canadian Society for the Study of Education
    Journal Abbr Canadian Journal of Education / Revue canadienne de l'éducation
    DOI 10.2307/1602201
    Accessed 10/4/2013, 4:32:53 PM
    Library Catalog JSTOR
    Abstract "Viewing and Representing in the Middle Years" was a two-year project to investigate visual literacy in the English language arts classrooms of three teachers. These teachers tried a variety of approaches and were generally optimistic about the benefits of the increased inclusion of visual materials. They did, however, report a number of challenges in using viewing and representing approaches as part of their curriculum. Teachers' previous experiences influenced their implementation of an expanded notion of literacy in English language arts, as did the influence of the university-based researcher conducting this investigation. /// L'article porte sur la visualisation et la représentation dans les premières années du secondaire dans les cours d'arts langagiers en anglais. Trois enseignants ont essayé diverses approches. Bien qu'ils voyaient d'un bon œil le fait d'inclure davantage de matériel visuel, ils ont signalé plusieurs difficultés reliées à l'utilisation d'approches de visualisation et de représentation dans leurs programmes. Les expériences antérieures des enseignants, tout comme la présente recherche, ont exercé une influence sur leur façon d'implanter une notion élargie de littératie dans les arts langagiers en anglais.

  • 'Visual literacy' as challenge to the internationalisation of interfaces: A study of South African student web users

    Type Conference Paper
    Author Marion Walton
    Author Vera Vukovic
    Author Gary Marsden
    Publisher Association for Computing Machinery
    Pages 530-531
    Date 2002
    Extra Conference Proceedings
    Accessed 1/1/2002, 4:00:00 PM
    Conference Name Conference on Human Factors in Computing Systems, April 20, 2002 - April 25
    Abstract Following a social semiotic approach, this paper questions the Western cultural assumptions underpinning the web's evolving navigational conventions, and investigates to what extent a group of South African students command the currently dominant Western conventions. South African students (both novices and experienced web users) completed a series of visual exercises, where they interpreted a set of interface and conceptual conventions in common use on the web. Conceptual questions attempted to address to what extent students were familiar with and able to reproduce the conventional Western visual design resources for representing classificational taxonomies or 'tree structures' and various other visual devices for the implicit portrayal of hierarchical information structures (Kress and van Leeuwen 1996). Interface questions probed student recognition of common web icons. Some broadly cultural factors were found to explain at least some of the variation in the group. Finally, we consider the implications of our study for training, design, and the diverse range of South African representational resources.
    Date Added 1/10/2013, 6:01:07 PM
    Modified 1/7/2016, 4:44:09 PM

  • The history of photographic education in Rochester, New York, 1960--1980

    Type Thesis
    Author Nancy M. Stuart
    URL http://search.proquest.com.ezproxy.library.ubc.ca/docview/305364892/abstract?accountid=14656
    Rights Copyright UMI - Dissertations Publishing 2005
    Place United States -- New York
    Date 2005
    Extra Historical Context
    Accessed 11/1/2013, 10:35:25 PM
    Library Catalog ProQuest
    Type Ph.D.
    Language English
    University State University of New York at Buffalo
    Abstract The history of photographic education will be discussed through case studies of two degree granting institutions: The Visual Studies Workshop and Rochester Institute of Technology's School of Photographic Arts and Sciences. Both programs were founded in Rochester, NY and have had major impact on the field of photography at a time when the discipline was relatively new to college campuses. Rochester has long been considered a center of American photography with its roots established by the Eastman Kodak Company founded in 1878. The area provided the infrastructure necessary for a new and growing industry: skilled labor, room to expand and ample transportation. The formation of companies like Bausch and Lomb in 1883 and Xerox Corporation in 1906 made Rochester a center for technological innovation in imaging. At various times since 1878, Rochester has been home to a total of twenty-three camera manufacturers, ten lens companies and eight photographic films, plates and paper manufacturers. 1 RIT and VSW represent divergent approaches to the teaching of college level photography, yet they developed in the same environment and experienced major enrollment growth at the same time. Nationally, Dr. C. William Horrell reported a total of twenty-five degree programs in photography, graduating a total of 143 students per year in 1964. 2 Thirty years later, the Focal Encyclopedia of Photography listed three hundred schools offering undergraduate degrees and sixty offering graduate degrees in photography. 3 Through oral histories the perspective of twenty-two faculty members and alumni were collected and analyzed including Anne Wilkes Tucker, Nathan and Joan Lyons, Carl Chiarenza, Richard Zakia, Robert Hirsch, and Les Stroebel. Materials from both schools' archives were researched including catalogs, campus newspapers, and marketing materials. No written history exists on Rochester's contributions to the development of photographic education. The coalescence of these two schools and Rochester organizations such as The George Eastman House, Kodak, the founding of The Society for Photographic Education, and Light Impressions Corporation contributed to the city being later dubbed the "imaging capital of the world." This dissertation considers the histories of two unique institutions, one representing technical education and the other, alternative education at a time when many university photography programs were experiencing major growth.
    # of Pages 196
    Date Added 11/1/2013, 10:35:25 PM
    Modified 1/7/2016, 7:52:30 PM

  • A Global imperative: The Report of the 21st century Literacy Summit

    Type Book
    Author The New Media Consortium
    URL http://www.adobe.com/education/pdf/globalimperative.pdf
    Publisher Adobe Systems Incorporated
    Date 2005
    Accessed 4/28/2014, 5:30:21 PM
    Abstract This body of research addresses the connection between arts, identity and new technology, and investigates the impact of images on adolescent identities, the relationship between online modes of communication and cyber-bullying, the increasing visualization of information and explores the way drawing and critical analysis of imagery develops visual literacy. Commissioned by Adobe Systems Pty Ltd, Australia (2003) to compile the Visual Literacy White Paper, Bamford’s report defines visual literacy and highlights its importance in the learning of such skill as problem solving and critical thinking. Providing strategies to promote visual literacy and emphasizing the role of technology in visual communication, this report has become a major reference for policy on visual literacy and cyber-bullying in the UK, USA and Asia.

  • Image Research and Use in the Humanities: An Idiosyncratic Bibliographic Essay

    Type Journal Article
    Author Katharine Martinez
    URL http://www.jstor.org.ezproxy.library.ubc.ca/stable/27949504
    Rights Copyright © 2009 The University of Chicago Press
    Volume 28
    Issue 1
    Pages 9-15
    Publication Art Documentation: Journal of the Art Libraries Society of North America
    ISSN 0730-7187
    Date April 1, 2009
    Extra 00001
    Journal Abbr Art Documentation: Journal of the Art Libraries Society of North America
    Accessed 4/3/2015, 2:04:16 AM
    Library Catalog JSTOR
    Abstract Thanks to Google, YouTube, and Facebook, finding images is easier than ever. Students and scholars surround themselves and their environments with images, yet when they use them in research, learning, teaching, and publishing, in most cases images play a secondary role to texts in supporting an argument. How can librarians teach users to bring their critical analysis skills to image research? What is visual literacy? This article offers an overview of the current trends and challenges in image research and usage in the field of American history, presents models of good practice, and discusses major academic initiatives to teach visual literacy.
    Short Title Image Research and Use in the Humanities

  • A Model Of Visual Perception Useful For Designers And Artists

    Type Journal Article
    Author Paula Csillag
    URL http://search.ebscohost.com/login.aspx?direct=true&db=ehh&AN=47220631&site=ehost-live&scope=site

    Abstract The purpose of this research was to develop a framework that would take into account the relevant findings from the psychological approaches to perception, both experimental as well as physiological, including the most recent findings on neuroscience, and unite these approaches with the traditional visual literacy approaches used in art and design. With such a framework, artists, designers, advertisers, and professionals dealing with images, can differentiate concepts related to visual perception and visual literacy from those concepts that cannot be generalized to all human beings, with the research background of scientific data.

  • The Eyes Know It? Training the Eyes: A Theory of Visual Literacy

    Type Journal Article
    Author Ari Santas
    Author Lisa Eaker
    URL http://search.ebscohost.com/login.aspx?direct=true&db=ehh&AN=47220633&site=ehost-live&scope=site
    Volume 28
    Issue 2
    Pages 163-185
    Publication Journal of Visual Literacy
    ISSN 1051144X
    Date October 2009
    Call Number 47220633
    Journal Abbr Journal of Visual Literacy
    Accessed 7/9/2013, 5:22:59 PM
    Library Catalog EBSCOhost
    Abstract This paper offers an analysis of the nature of perception and its relationship to thinking, using the psychology and epistemology of C.S. Peirce and John Dewey. It is our contention that the cultivation of creativity and critical thinking are hampered by a false epistemological dichotomy between perceiving and thinking and its attending educational dualism of thinking/training. There is a growing awareness among many researchers that points to the continuity between thinking and perceiving with work that highlights how perceptual judgments are used both with great skill and benefit in some cases and grave consequences in others; the difference between these uses has everything to do with training. Experts know what to look for and what to screen out; non-experts can be confused by too much and/or irrelevant information present in the perceptual field. Our purpose is to explore this research, explicate this continuity and to suggest some prescriptions for teaching visual literacy. To this end, we shall propose a "taxonomy of training," with special focus on visual imagery
    Short Title The Eyes Know It?

  • The Rhetorical Genre in Graphic Design: Its Relationship to Design Authorship and Implications to Design Education

    Type Journal Article
    Author Cristina De Almeida
    URL http://search.ebscohost.com/login.aspx?direct=true&db=ufh&AN=47220630&site=ehost-live&scope=site
    Volume 28
    Issue 2
    Pages 186-198
    Publication Journal of Visual Literacy
    ISSN 1051144X
    Date October 2009
    Journal Abbr Journal of Visual Literacy
    Accessed 3/17/2014, 4:52:24 PM
    Library Catalog EBSCOhost
    Abstract The notion of rhetorical genre in graphic design will also be explored in this paper. Four discursive functions encompassing a range of possibilities for visual/verbal communication will be located: summation, juxtaposition, narration, and exposition. Recurrent combinations of these functions within specific graphic formats and styles help to constitute a visual/verbal vocabulary of typified features, determined in part by cultural and cognitive factors. Designers may either work within this generic framework or challenge its boundaries in response to specific situational demands. A higher degree of authorship in graphic design takes place when designers can control the usage of specific discursive functions and critically engage rhetorical genres in the articulation of visual/verbal arguments.
    Short Title The Rhetorical Genre in Graphic Design

  • Antinomies of Semiotics in Graphic Design

    Type Journal Article
    Author Peter Storkerson
    URL http://search.ebscohost.com/login.aspx?direct=true&db=aft&AN=505284247&site=ehost-live&scope=site
    Volume 44
    Issue 1
    Pages 5-37
    Publication Part of a special issue: Communication Design Failures
    ISSN 00222224
    Date January 2010
    Journal Abbr Visible Language
    Accessed 7/30/2014, 4:29:05 PM
    Library Catalog EBSCOhost
    Abstract The following paper assesses the roles played by semiotics in graphic design and in graphic design education, which both reflects and shapes practice. It identifies a series of factors; graphic design education methods and culture; semiotic theories themselves and their application to graphic design; the two wings of Peircian semiotics and Saussurian semiology and their incompatibilities; semiology's linguocentrism, its affinity to cultural criticism and its seminal role in cultural and social anthropology, structuralism, poststructuralism and deconstruction. It examines the uses and criticisms of semiotics and semiology in design, their use in graphic design education, and their operationalization within technical communication and human factors as paths that might be applied to graphic design.

  • Reading Multimodal Texts: Perceptual, Structural and Ideological Perspectives

    Type Journal Article
    Author Frank Serafini
    URL http://link.springer.com.ezproxy.library.ubc.ca/article/10.1007/s10583-010-9100-5
    Volume 41
    Issue 2
    Pages 85-104
    Publication Children's Literature in Education
    ISSN 0045-6713, 1573-1693
    Date 2010/04/02
    Extra 00064
    Journal Abbr Child Lit Educ
    DOI 10.1007/s10583-010-9100-5
    Accessed 1/7/2016, 8:40:21 PM
    Library Catalog link.springer.com.ezproxy.library.ubc.ca
    Language en
    Abstract This article presents a tripartite framework for analyzing multimodal texts. The three analytical perspectives presented include: (1) perceptual, (2) structural, and (3) ideological analytical processes. Using Anthony Browne’s picturebook Piggybook as an example, assertions are made regarding what each analytical perspective brings to the interpretation of multimodal texts and how these perspectives expand readers’ interpretive repertoires. Drawing on diverse fields of inquiry, including semiotics, art theory, visual grammar, communication studies, media literacy, visual literacy and literary theory, the article suggests an expansion of the strategies and analytical perspectives readers being to multimodal texts and visual images. Each perspective is presented as necessary but insufficient in and of itself to provide the necessary foundation for comprehending texts. It is through an expansion of the interpretive strategies and perspectives that readers bring to a multimodal text, focusing on visual, textual, and design elements that readers will become more proficient in their interpretive processes.
    Short Title Reading Multimodal Texts
    Date Added 1/7/2016, 8:40:21 PM
    Modified 1/8/2016, 10:50:50 AM

  • The levels of visual framing

    Type Journal Article
    Author Lulu Rodriguez
    Author Daniela V. Dimitrova
    URL http://search.ebscohost.com/login.aspx?direct=true&db=ehh&AN=61341520&site=ehost-live&scope=site
    Volume 30
    Issue 1
    Pages 48-65
    Publication Journal of Visual Literacy
    ISSN 1051144X
    Date Spring 2011
    Call Number 61341520
    Journal Abbr Journal of Visual Literacy
    Accessed 7/9/2013, 5:19:27 PM
    Library Catalog EBSCOhost
    Abstract While framing research has centered mostly on the evaluations of media texts, visual news discourse has remained relatively unexamined. This study surveys the visual framing techniques and methods employed in previous studies and proposes a four-tiered model of identifying and analyzing visual frames: (1) visuals as denotative systems, (2) visuals as stylistic-semiotic systems, (3) visuals as connotative systems and (4) visuals as ideological representations. These four tiers are defined and the process of identifying frames at each level is explicated. The proposed system can be applied to analyzing any type of visual media content or audiences' perception of that content.
    Date Added 7/9/2013, 5:19:27 PM
    Modified 1/8/2016, 12:23:29 AM

  • Toward a Cohesive Theory of Visual Literacy

    Type Journal Article
    Author Maria D. Avgerinou
    Author Rune Pettersson
    URL http://search.ebscohost.com/login.aspx?direct=true&db=ufh&AN=82584418&site=ehost-live&scope=site
    Volume 30
    Issue 2
    Pages 1-19
    Publication Journal of Visual Literacy
    ISSN 1051144X
    Date October 2011
    Journal Abbr Journal of Visual Literacy
    Accessed 4/29/2014, 12:37:05 AM
    Library Catalog EBSCOhost
    Abstract Despite the fact that to date Visual Literacy (VL) scholars have not arrived at a general consensus for a theoretical organization of the VL field, important conceptual investigations have emerged over the past four decades. In this paper we discuss and synthesize those studies. We then present a first attempt toward a cohesive theory of VL. The main components of the proposed VL theory are Visual Perception, Visual Language (ViL), Visual Learning, Visual Thinking, and Visual Communication. Here we focus on discussing ViL and its constituent parts: 1) ViL exists; 2) ViL is holistic; 3) ViL must be learned; 3) ViL may improve learning; 5) ViL is not universal; 6) ViL often needs verbal support.
    Date Added 4/29/2014, 12:37:05 AM
    Modified 1/8/2016, 10:35:40 AM

  • Visual “Literacy” in the Digital Age

    Type Journal Article
    Author Paul Messaris
    URL http://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/15358593.2011.653508
    Volume 12
    Issue 2
    Pages 101-117
    Publication Review of Communication
    ISSN null
    Date 2012
    Extra 00001
    DOI 10.1080/15358593.2011.653508
    Accessed 11/1/2013, 2:34:17 PM
    Library Catalog Taylor and Francis+NEJM
    Abstract This essay is a reflection on the ways in which visual media have evolved since the 1994 publication of the author's book Visual “Literacy”: Image, Mind, and Reality. The essay focuses on two major technological changes in visual media, and on the cultural transformations that have accompanied those changes. The first technological change is the development of increasingly powerful techniques for the digital manipulation of photographs and the creation of computer-generated photorealistic images. Reviewing this development, which has made the task of detecting visual fraud more challenging, the essay assesses the likelihood that it may also have brought about an enhancement in societal levels of visual “literacy.” Such an enhancement may also be a by-product of the second technological change discussed in this essay, namely, the increasing availability of digital networks for the dissemination of images and for public commentary about those images. The essay examines the use of such commentary for questioning the authenticity of visual advocacy and for exposing digital fraud.
    Date Added 11/1/2013, 2:34:17 PM
    Modified 1/8/2016, 2:51:42 PM

  • Toward a Shared Vocabulary for Visual Analysis: An Analytic Toolkit for Deconstructing the Visual Design of Graphic Novels

    Type Journal Article
    Author Sean P. Connors
    URL http://search.ebscohost.com/login.aspx?direct=true&db=ehh&AN=77689803&site=ehost-live&scope=site
    Volume 31
    Issue 1
    Pages 71-91
    Publication Journal of Visual Literacy
    ISSN 1051144X
    Date Spring 2012
    Call Number 77689803
    Journal Abbr Journal of Visual Literacy
    Accessed 7/9/2013, 5:14:52 PM
    Library Catalog EBSCOhost
    Abstract Literacy educators might advocate using graphic novels to develop students' visual literacy skills, but teachers who lack a vocabulary for engaging in close analysis of visual texts may be reluctant to teach them. Recognizing this, teacher educators should equip preservice teachers with a vocabulary for analyzing visual texts. This article foregrounds three analytic concepts preservice teachers can use to examine the role issues of design play in eliciting their response to images--specifically, those in graphic novels. To demonstrate how the concepts function, the author applies them to his own reading of panels excerpted from a critically acclaimed graphic novel for adolescents.
    Short Title Toward a Shared Vocabulary for Visual Analysis

  • Visual Literacy and the Untimely Transmogrification of the Problem

    Type Journal Article
    Author Jessie Beier
    URL http://muse.jhu.edu.ezproxy.library.ubc.ca/journals/visual_arts_research/v039/39.1.beier.html
    Volume 39
    Issue 1
    Pages 35-51
    Publication Visual Arts Research
    ISSN 2151-8009
    Date 2013
    Extra <p>Volume 39, Number 1, Issue 76, Summer 2013</p>
    Accessed 2/25/2014, 12:24:21 AM
    Library Catalog Project MUSE
    Abstract AbstractIt seems as though we have come to the understanding that we live in an increasingly visual world. In educational contexts, the notion that ours is an image-saturated existence has led to a focus on how to best equip learners with the skills needed to participate within this world. Visual literacy, or the question of "how people perceive objects, interpret what they see, and what they learn from them" (Elkins, 2008, p. 2), has been brought to the forefront as one strategy that works to meet the demands of our contemporary moment. This work investigates the potential problem of visual literacy, particularly in relation to art (and its) education. Drawing on the work of contemporary visual artist Chris Millar, this investigation employs a Deleuzoguattarian conceptual toolkit in order to transmogrify a current understanding of the art encounter based on a visual literacy approach. Instead, this exploration develops a philosophy of fabulation in order to think art and our encounter with it in a manner delinked from representation, ultimately asking the question What does art do?
    Date Added 2/25/2014, 12:24:21 AM
    Modified 1/8/2016, 2:58:34 PM

  • A Big Picture Approach: Using Embedded Librarianship to Proactively Address the Need for Visual Literacy Instruction in Higher Education

    Type Journal Article
    Author Amanda Milbourn
    URL http://www.jstor.org.ezproxy.library.ubc.ca/stable/10.1086/673517
    Volume 32
    Issue 2
    Pages 274-283
    Publication Art Documentation: Journal of the Art Libraries Society of North America
    ISSN 0730-7187
    Date 2013
    Extra 00001
    Journal Abbr Art Documentation: Journal of the Art Libraries Society of North America
    DOI 10.1086/673517
    Accessed 1/7/2016, 8:38:24 PM
    Library Catalog JSTOR
    Abstract AbstractAs images become ubiquitous and more accessible in digital culture, their role in the creation and dissemination of knowledge across academic disciplines is growing. Academic institutions need to adapt to this change by introducing new skill sets into the undergraduate curriculum. The term visual literacy encompasses the competencies necessary to critically use, produce, and analyze images. This article surveys the current methods academic librarians are using to introduce visual literacy instruction within their institutions. Each surveyed model focuses on a specific stakeholder group: students, faculty, and librarians. The article advocates for a method of instruction that combines the discussed approaches and stakeholders into a single framework by utilizing embedded librarianship practices.
    Short Title A Big Picture Approach

  • Visual Literacy and the Digital Native: Another Look

    Type Journal Article
    Author Richard Emanuel
    Author Siu Challons-Lipton
    URL http://search.ebscohost.com/login.aspx?direct=true&db=ufh&AN=99562937&site=ehost-live&scope=site
    Volume 32
    Issue 1
    Pages 7-26
    Publication Journal of Visual Literacy
    ISSN 1051144X
    Date Spring 2013
    Extra 00004
    Journal Abbr Journal of Visual Literacy
    Accessed 1/7/2016, 8:34:33 PM
    Library Catalog EBSCOhost
    Abstract A conceptualization of visual proficiency is offered. A survey is described which asked college students (N=358) to identify five photographs, five symbols, and five paintings that are generally recognized to be "famous". About 27% of all respondents claimed to recognize all the photographs, 23% recognized all the symbols, and 3% recognized all the paintings. About 14% correctly identified all the photographs, 4% correctly identified all the symbols and only 1% correctly identified all the paintings. The findings suggest that today's college students may be adrift in a sea of images with little ability to see beyond their own generation.
    Short Title Visual Literacy and the Digital Native
    Date Added 1/7/2016, 8:34:33 PM
    Modified 1/8/2016, 3:02:44 PM

  • Cognitive Visual Literacy: From Theories and Competencies to Pedagogy

    Type Journal Article
    Author Nicole A. Beatty
    URL http://www.jstor.org/stable/10.1086/669987
    Rights Copyright © 2013 The University of Chicago Press
    Volume 32
    Issue 1
    Pages 33-42
    Publication Art Documentation: Journal of the Art Libraries Society of North America
    ISSN 0730-7187
    Date March 1, 2013
    Extra ArticleType: research-article / Full publication date: Spring 2013 / Copyright © 2013 The University of Chicago Press
    Journal Abbr Art Documentation: Journal of the Art Libraries Society of North America
    DOI 10.1086/669987
    Accessed 10/4/2013, 4:14:31 PM
    Library Catalog JSTOR
    Abstract Abstract Visual literacy is an important part of being literate in the twenty-first century because people are interacting with visual materials with increasing frequency and immediacy as a result of the digital age. By understanding cognitive theories associated with visual literacy and combining them with the ACRL Visual Literacy Competency Standards for Higher Education, librarians can help students become visually literate. In addition to describing these cognitive theories and ACRL standards, the author provides practical suggestions as to how they may be utilized in visual literacy instruction.

  • Promoting visual literacy among undergraduate students in geography: teaching a visualized Latin America

    Type Journal Article
    Author Verónica Hollman
    URL http://dx.doi.org/10.1080/03098265.2013.836626
    Volume 38
    Issue 1
    Pages 136-147
    Publication Journal of Geography in Higher Education
    ISSN 0309-8265
    Date January 2, 2014
    Extra 00000
    DOI 10.1080/03098265.2013.836626
    Accessed 3/19/2015, 11:14:23 PM
    Library Catalog Taylor and Francis+NEJM
    Abstract This study offers a discussion of the role of the visual in the professional training of geographers arguing that visual literacy is not necessarily promoted during geography undergraduate studies. It then analyzes an experience of teaching a visualized Latin America developed in Argentina, as an illustrative example: on the one hand, it reveals the general lack of visual literacy among geography students; on the other hand, it suggests strategies that call for a shift in what is classically understood as a technical skill. It concludes addressing the need to redefine visual literacy in broader terms within the discipline and its training.
    Short Title Promoting visual literacy among undergraduate students in geography

  • Geographic Media Literacy

    Type Journal Article
    Author Chris Lukinbeal
    URL http://dx.doi.org/10.1080/00221341.2013.846395
    Volume 113
    Issue 2
    Pages 41-46
    Publication Journal of Geography
    ISSN 0022-1341
    Date March 4, 2014
    Extra 00001
    DOI 10.1080/00221341.2013.846395
    Accessed 3/19/2015, 11:16:40 PM
    Library Catalog Taylor and Francis+NEJM
    Abstract While the use of media permeates geographic research and pedagogic practice, the underlying literacies that link geography and media remain uncharted. This article argues that geographic media literacy incorporates visual literacy, information technology literacy, information literacy, and media literacy. Geographic media literacy is the ability to locate, evaluate, effectively use, and produce geographic information. It is associated with analysis and expression, understanding and praxis. In an era where information increasingly comes from media sources and technologies saturate everyday life, media and media-related technologies have become central to geographic literacy.

  • Handbook of Research on Teaching Literacy Through the Communicative and Visual Arts, Volume II: A Project of the International Reading Association

    Type Book
    Author James Flood
    Author Shirley Brice Heath
    Author Diane Lapp
    Publisher Routledge
    ISBN 978-1-317-63970-1
    Date 2015-04-22
    Extra ⬚⬚
    Library Catalog Google Books
    Language en
    Abstract The Handbook of Research on Teaching Literacy Through the Communicative and Visual Arts, Volume II brings together state-of-the-art research and practice on the evolving view of literacy as encompassing not only reading, writing, speaking, and listening, but also the multiple ways through which learners gain access to knowledge and skills. It forefronts as central to literacy education the visual, communicative, and performative arts, and the extent to which all of the technologies that have vastly expanded the meanings and uses of literacy originate and evolve through the skills and interests of the young. A project of the International Reading Association, published and distributed by Routledge/Taylor & Francis. Visit http://www.reading.org for more information about Internationl Reading Associationbooks, membership, and other services.
